I dont really care much about historical accuracy in movies since they usually prioritize artistic value (or financial greed nowadays) over anything else. If anyone gets their History from Movies, thats their fault to begin with I would say.  Having said that, the depiction of Colour Sergeant Bourne in the Movie is truly remarkable and a great example of what an NCO should be. He is stern, disciplined and has a good eye for things that might cause trouble. One of the often overlooked parts where he plays a crucial role is when the Cavalry abandons them and the men are mortified and look in horror as they ride away. It is Colour Sergeant Bourne that breaks the tension with what seems to be extremely mundane given the circumstances when he says "get back to work" but his timing is impeccable and possibly averts despair taking a hold of the men by sending them back to duties and away from thinking too much about it.  Another scene is when the Soldier, visibly shaken, that guards the Priest is being subjected to the sermons of the Priest raising doubts in him. The Colour Sergeant gives him a stern look bringing him back in line and then (incredible performance by the actor) tells the Priest to basically "STFU" in a way that seems elegant and well spoken but at the same time carries over a silent but definitive menace/threat which is generally a part of the depiction of the Character throughout the Movie.  When he says "Now there is a good Gentlemen", you understand that what he is really saying is "do or Im gonna rip you a new one" and you believe he will.

I wrote a paper on the Battle of Biak that occurred during WWII. It was an incredible story and I think few know of it. It was just a grueling tropical summer on a coral cliff shored island that was home to three Japanese Airfields. Fighting in the South Pacific was like traveling to the moon to fight. There was little in the way of supply or maintenance. I know that General McArthur's army contained Australian sailors and airmen. So any involvement of those sailors and airmen in that battle, that would make a good video. A eldest son of the of President Theodore Roosevelt, Lieutenant Colonel Theodore Roosevelt Jr., was pinned down under the coral cliffs with the 162nd Infantry Regiment which had to be repositioned by LVTs and DUKWs. Royal Australian Navy ships participated in the Allied task force, included: - HMAS Australia (heavy cruiser) - HMAS Shropshire (heavy cruiser) - HMAS Warramunga (destroyer) - Australian air squadrons also contributed to strategic air support, operating from bases as far away as Darwin.

Color Sergeant Bourne was awarded the Victoria Cross. He chose to receive a Commission as an Officer in the 24th Foot in lieu of the VC. Bourne retired a Lt Colonel of the Regiment and famously recorded much of his exploits on tape speaking to the BBC. Unfortunately, during the Blitz the recordings were lost, That's what happened to Lt Col Bourne.

Even in Welington's heyday about one in twenty British officers were 'Johnny Jump-Ups', commissioned rankers. But few got as far as a captaincy. The mid- and late Victorian military reforms facilitated Bourne's rise. The most spectacular example of the gradual move towards French-style meritocratic promotion was Wiliam Robertson: enlisted as a private, retired as field marshal and Chief of the Imperial General Staff. 'Wullie' commanded the largest army in UK history during the Great War. His career would be impossible under today's regulations.

The main thing with the Victoria cross whether accurate or not depicted in the movie was the majority that fought that were in sickbay and fought actively in the battles. Richard Burton narration at the end explains this. Victoria cross was created for valour and extreme courage not normally expected of the British soldier in face of the enemy. Where the majority were injured in battle or were already injured or sick fought on.

As a junior officer in training, we watched this film. We were then, after, asked by the instructor [s] to write an after action report with appropriate recommendations for action taken under fire.  Everyone thought Bourne had performed admirably and expressed curiosity as to why he was not also recommended for a VC. And, yes, the instructor's response was the same - he was just doing his job.  Given what you have found and covered in this video, I think that if he had accepted the commission offered there would have been no question about him getting the VC. The fact that he WAS awarded the DCM AND an annuity equal to the VC's rather says it all. At that time in the British Army, commissions for officer rank were bought, and not generally earned. That he was offered one is indicative of the regard in which he was held. A commission as a lieutant would have normally cost Â£700 pounds sterling. If he was being asked to purchase it, then you can readily see why he declined.

How extraordinary that he lived to the end of WWII.  Early in the war, as a morale booster, the British published a series of pamphlets under the general heading "Great Exploits".  I still have the one titled "The Day of Rourke's Drift".  I read it many years before the movie was made, so I was eager to see how the event would be portrayed.  Except that there was more fighting at night than the movie suggested, I found it reasonably accurate.   It is amazing that Bourne lived to see the story published so many years later and at a time Britain needed to know the story.  I'll have to read it again to see whether there are references to him.

Wonderful video.  My step father was a fan of miniature soldiers.  He spent hours painting them.  One of his collections was of this battle.  He also had two lithographs art pieces depicting this battle.  We watch the two movies, Zulu and Zulu Dawn multiple times together.  He was a career soldier in the US Army (serving in post war Europe in the mid fifties, after serving in Korea, and in the 60's, in Viet Nam) and an amateur historian.  The British Empire always held a fascination for him, especially the First World War.  He had memorized many of Rudyard Kiplings most famous poems, including Gunga Din, The Road to Mandalay, and Tommy.  Thanks for the memories...I lost my dad in 2018.

Thanks for this great presentation on CSGT Bourne. First seen by me in a high school english class, while I was also in the school's cadet unit. He is the epitome of the Senior NCO I later encountered in my Australian Army Reserve service, and the type of SNCO I wanted to be when I attained the rank of Sergeant. In later times I served as a volunteer fire service officer for 30 years, where this calm succinct voice of authority I believe was key in leading firefighters and assisting worried fire victims. The calm succinct voice of authority and firefighters who never once flinched servived the day.

Don't ever take the film 'Zulu' as a history lesson. The film bears little relation to the facts. The film gives the impression that it was a regiment made up of mostly Welsh soldiers, yet it was actually an English regiment, made up of mostly English soldiers. The overemphasising of the Welsh element was no doubt due to Stanley Baker's involvement, even including fellow Welshman Richard Burton as the narrator. The Zulus didn't retreat in recognition of the soldier's bravery in holding out against them, they retreated because they knew a regiment of soldiers was making its way to Rourke's Drift.  The film is a great example of a great adventure story, very loosely based on an actual event. If you want the true account of what actually happened, then you'd be better served by looking elsewhere.

There were survivors from Isandlwana, though few if any of them wore the famous red tunic. One Horace Lockwood Smith-Dorrien, then a second lieutenant, was one of only five officers to escape, attributing his survival in part to his blue, rather than red tunic. His survival would prove fortunate for more than himself as the high point of Smith-Dorrienâ€™s career was to come during the long retreat to the Marne river ordered by Field Marshal Sir John French in 1914. Smith-Dorrien had become by then a full General and at Le Cateau, on 26 August he was forced to halt his column, reportedly commenting, â€œVery, well, gentleman, we will fightâ€. It was a decision taken to prevent the Germans from overwhelming the column on the march. A call from BEF HQ, ordering him to continue the retreat; was refused, as the men were already fighting. This rear-guard action stalled the German advance saving the British Army from a much larger force.

A final anecdote from the Zulu film isn't actually from Colour Sergeant Bourne this time, but from Stanley Baker in his role of Lieutenant Chard - and is rather sad than amusing. After the battle, both Chard and Bromhead (played by the brilliant Michael Caine) were discussing the whole bloody affair with regret and sadness when Chard utters his unforgettable final words in the film - "And I came here to build a bridge" - a truly melancholy statement after the horrible bloodshed both officers and their men had just been through, but had not chosen for themselves or indeed, for their Zulu enemies who lay dead around Rorke's Drift.

Great video! Only correction: Bourne was 5'6", albeit still nearly a foot shorter than Nigel Green. Of interesting note: The second highest-ranking NCO at Rorke's Drift, Sergeant Henry Gallagher (who's not in the film) was just twenty-three. Of the remaining section leaders, who were all junior to Gallagher in date-of-rank, Sergeant George Smith was thirty-nine, Sergeant Joseph Windridge was around thirty-five, with the recently demoted to corporal William Allan the same. Corporal Alfred Saxty, who was promoted to sergeant the day after the battle to replace the badly injured Allan (who still held a sergeant's billet), became the youngest section leader at just nineteen. Most of the private soldiers under their charge would have ranged between eighteen and twenty-one years of age.  I personally think Nigel Green would have been better cast as Commissary Dalton, who gets arguably the worst depiction of the film, outside of Henry Hook's character assassination. The real Dalton was 6' tall (so a giant for the time) thickly muscled and a highly experienced retired Army Senior NCO. Peter Gill, who gave a respectable depiction of Private John Williams (Fielding), VC, would have been a more accurate casting choice for Bourne in terms of age. Still, Zulu is a great film despite its many inaccuracies, and is what got most of us interested in the Zulu War. When writing my own novels on the conflict (which to date is the only series to cover the entirety of the war) I still gave a nod to the film by giving some of Nigel Green's film lines to my version of Bourne.

I served in the U.S. Marines as a helicopter gunship pilot through two tours in Vietnam.  I have read all the accounts I could find of the Battle of Rorke's Drift, starting with the account in "Our Empire Story" when I was a boy of twelve.  And, I have watched the movie "Zulu" multiple times.  I have always thought that Rorke's Drift was the best example of what properly led soldiers can do, even in the worst of battlefield conditions.  From the accounts I have read, and from the movie, I think Color Sgt. Bourne epitomized everything that a non-commissioned officer should be.  A truly magnificent soldier!

As a Met Police officer in the Brixton riots of the 1980s, our small unit was suddenly about to be swamped by a huge chanting and charging crowd of black faced rioters. To my surprise, initial fear and apprehension turned to complete, sheer bloody anger, and a clear desire to take as many of them with me as I could before I went down. Weirdly, at that moment, Rorkes Drift and the resolution of the defenders came to my mind - except that this time it was us with the shields and sticks whilst they had the projectiles. Thankfully they stopped short and relied on raining missiles on to us rather than physically engage us. Shortly, a relief force arrived and we drove them back.  The experience stuck with me of course, and I felt grateful for the feeling that I had had the very slightest, but still significant, connection with what our brave forebears had experienced!

We visited Rorke's Drift in September 1995. We stayed at The Penny Farthing, not far from Rorke's Drift. The owner was an historian and gave us lots of information. We traveled to Rorke's Drift and were surprised by the lack of much there to remember the event. The museum (we were told by the curator) had been open about three years. There was little else there. No hotels or shops. There was a diorama of the scene in the museum. From memory, the notes with it stated that almost 20,000 rounds had been fired, which was almost all of the ammunition held. In my view, I think there would have been more Zulu casualties. The defenders would have been firing at   short range or point blank. I accept that there would have been misses, but accounting for the several hundred wounded, you still have maybe 16,000 bullets that failed to find their mark. We will return at some time, although the site has changed greatly since we were there.
Brilliantly presented, I really enjoyed the story of Frank Bourne an amazing and courageous soldier, who achieved so much in his long life. I joined the army aged 15 years in 1960 and met many men who reminded me of Colour Sergeant Frank Bourne, their manner and their bearing. Many of those I knew had served during WWII and were coming to end of their service and in some ways laid the blueprint of how we, in later service, would conduct ourselves. I am now a retired British Army Officer.

In the film, the two commissioned officers are often excited and angry but Colour Sergeant Bourne never loses his composure.  Even during his bayonet fight, one of the most dramatic moments in a film with many such moments, he is in complete control.  Compare his movements and facial expression with that of the young Zulu warrior he is fighting.  His greater skill and self-confidence, the result of years of training and combat experience, are obvious.  Like the sword fight near the beginning of the Seven Samurai, there is never any doubt about who will win.

As for Bourne being the son of a farm labourer, but despite this was literate, a surprising number of 19th century farm labourers were literate ... more so than lower class city dwellers  Their work was seasonal and they had time on their hands when not working to educate themselves.

Like many, I was very taken with Nigel Greene's performance and his absolute personification of the Colour Sergeant. What I most remember about it was a scene in which he bayonets a Zulu warrior and does it absolutely by the book - as you would expect from his character: thrust, twist, withdraw. he did it with no sign of fear or triumph, just doing with some distaste as was expected of him. As an aside, my mother and auntie went to the Royal Soldiers Daughters' school in Hampstead in the 1930s where they were classmates with Henry Hook's granddaughter. One other thing I will say is this. When I was an apprentice on the railways in the early-mid seventies I came into contact with an Scottish RSM, who like Bourne was a mere 5' 3". Frankly, the height didn't come into the equation. He was barrel chested, stood bolt upright and scared the daylights out of everyone. First thing he ever said to me was, 'What are you doing here laddy? Clear off.' I was actually there because I was meant to be there, but I went anyway.

Bourne's moustache and sideburns were called Burnsides, named after the US army general of the same name who had fought for the North in the civil war. When men stopped growing the moustache but kept the rest then the name was reversed and they became sideburns.  Stanley Baker owned Chard's original Victoria Cross, though he did not know that at the time. And, of course, he should have worn a blue coat being an engineer.  At the end of the film Michael Caine, playing Bromhead, tells Baker's Chard that he had not fought a battle before. This was not true as he already had combat experience.

My mothers family were Clan Gunn and her father went to South Africa to fight in the Boer War and remained in South Africa as both a Soldier and Colonial Administrator - He commanded a battalion of the Kings African Rifles recruited from Zulus in both WW1 fighting in both German East Africa and Germand South West Africa (now Namibia) against the Germans under Lettow Von Vorbeck and in Burma fighting the Japanese in WW2.     He had a farm on the west bank of the River Tugela opposite Zulu Land near where the Buffalo River (forded at Rorkes Drift) joined the Tugela River - The farm workers were the families of fallen Zulu Askaris from his battalion and his former Askaris and their families  by Zulu.     My mother grew up speaking both the Zulu language known as isiZulu and Xhosa.  I never knew my mothers parents as they dies before I was born BUT remember my mothers stories of growing up on the High Veldt and the family dogs, Rhodesian Ridgebacks trained to protect the family from predatory Lions and swimming in the River Tugela as children with her 2 brothers taking it in turns to sit on a rock with a high power rifle to shoot any predatory crocodiles !!!!     There were many stories lion kills on the house veranda, elephants stampeding through the house and stories of the Zulu farm workers and children including a graphic story of children scrabbling for dry wood to eat the termites from the wood and using their fingers to push termites crawling out of their mouths back into their mouths - Termites being a valued high protein snack!!  Of importance to this post is the fact my mothers father was a very strict military man and he always praised the Zulus he commanded in both world wars as the best soldiers he had ever fought with or commanded and the only soldiers fighting for the crown against the Germans in southern Africa that could out march and out fight the German army comprising 1000 Germans and 10,000 Askaris.      I assume that also applied to the previous generation of Zulus from the Zulu War who could run 40 miles to a battle and fight a battle when they got there!       A factor that meant once battle was joined only cavalry and mounted infantry could escape meaning any British or Boer force fighting the Zulus had to stand their ground and win as at Rorkes Drift where the soldiers had open ammunition cases unlimited ammunition or die as they did at Isandlwana when they ran out of ammunition because the ammunition was being fired faster than the ammunition case lids could be unscrewed and distributed.  From the comments my grandfather made I am sure they were the equal of the Gurkhas surprised the Army didn't recruit Zulu battalions like it recruited Gurkhas.
The battle scenes were not very realistic!
I saw Zulu in 1964 at the Odeon cinema in Southend-on-Sea High Street and even now can recall thinking that it was a film that would remain in my mind forever.  It was not just the story, which of course I realised then that it might not be totally accurate, but it was actually the film itself, the situation, the colours, the people, the interactions between characters like Chard and Bromhead where they fought to establish their seniority, that were so well written and directed and performed. Ten years later I was in South Africa in 1974, married, and discovered the bravery and toughness of the magnificent Zulu nation, where I employed them for their loyalty and strength of purpose and mind, with other tribes deferring to them always.  I think the film showed both sides well and was very respectful to the Zulu nation, and for me, the most haunting thing of all was when they withdrew their men and their assegais, with the sound of their chanting as a sign of respect to the people who defended Rorke's Drift just fading into the distance.  I have a vast collection of dvds, many quite superb and memorable, but which one do I watch time and again to see what I might have missed from the film over those almost sixty years!  Thank you Cy, and congratulations.

Unfortunately, and I am not tooting my own horn, there are fewer and fewer that realize that without those junior men and women? Our stripes are just pretty decorations. Fewer and fewer are those in the commissioned ranks that truly care for their troops. Too busy trying to climb that ladder. Too many cowards. God bless the professional NCO. The backbone of every unit there is and ever was. Nothing quite as terrifying as a NCO who knows what they're about, and no one else you'd rather have with you when the dying starts.

In 1967, I was detached from my regiment in Aden on a 3 month secondment in newly independent Botswana. It turned out the outfit I was seconded to was B Company, South Wales Borderers. And they remembered Rorkes Drift OK! There were days (especially after  few beers) when it seemed all you would hear was "11 VCs before breakfast". I swear I heard it so often it made my ears bleed! While in Botswana, the regiment decided it would be a grand day out for a party from the company to make a pilgrimage to Rorkes Drift (probably about 700 miles in Army Land Rovers and Bedford 3 tonners). On the way, they had to ford the Limpopo River; wide but shallow enough to ford. The CO called for a volunteer to walk ahead across the river to ensure they kept the convoy on the shallowest part. A Lance Corporal (pretty sure he was a Davies) volunteered. When they safely reached the other side, the CO called to Lance Corporal and said "Well done...do you know what that was?" No Sir. "It was the Limpopo River, and do you know what it is famous for?" No Sir. "It is famous for the biggest collection of crocodiles in southern Africa". L/Cpl reportedly fainted on the spot!

In the main the British Army was divided into families otherwise known as battalions or regiments etc These soldiers who lived and shared the good with the bad were well known for their complaining natures about anything and everything in the British Army, but when the shit hit the fan those same men became a deadly organised killing machine to be feared by all who opposed them: The British Army was more at war with itself in peacetime than it was during any conflict. British soldiers became bored very easily in peacetime so when a conflict was rammed down their throats they went at it as only a brave and disciplined army should. There were odd times when a few did let the side down but they were truly ostracized by their mates. The British army compared to some is a small army but it is also a mighty army when roused:

You asked so here goes.... After a gruelling Life&Death struggle lasting all night, the inevitable morning roll call has to be performed. Precious few are present. Colour Sergeant Bourne conducts the sombre proceedings stoically with a pencil. So many names are absent, presumed dead. He gets to Hitch. But Hitch doesn't answer. Bourne tuts and  says loudly, "Hitch, I've seen you... You're ALIVE.." Meekly you hear Hitch respond from somewhere in the pitifully sparce line of surviving troops. He replies boyishly... "Ah, thank you very much Sir..." CLASSIC LINE, MY FAVOURITE.  I WET MYSELF LAUGHING EVERYTIME. FOR ME, IT SUMS UP THE BATTLE PERFECTLY Ravi from London in Australia

In mid-1978, a handful of friends and I took an "unscheduled" holiday from our university studies to go down to Babanango in Natal, to be extras in the film "Zulu Dawn". This kindled a greater interest in the Anglo Zulu war and over the years I have gathered a fantastic set of books as well as original engravings from the Illustrated London News that depicted battle scenes of that ill-fated campaign. Both "Zulu" and "Zulu Dawn" were "generous" in their depiction of these two iconic battles - as is much of the historic record. Little is spoken of the bravery of individual Zulus- perhaps not surprising as they made no written record of their own tactics and heroes. In any event, it is a fascinating period in British Imperial History, and it's sad that schools both in SA and the UK do not make more of this campaign. Rorkes Drift is indeed one of the great defensive encounters of the 19th-century British Army. Very informative video... Thoroughly enjoyable!

I was given a pile of the prop uniforms when I ran a small military museum in the 80s in Essex. I thought I'd taken possession of a treasure trove until I opened the large box and discovered that the tunics had been stored in soaking wet conditions and were sodden and mouldy and stuck together, literally dripping with water. They actually fell apart as I tried to separate them. I seem to recall a couple of them had the actor's names written inside the collars, including Stanley Baker's. What a let-down.

I always felt that he didn't get the VC even though he deserved it because he was simply doing the job that was expected of him. Awarding military medals is a strange process. My father told the story of an air gunner in his squadron who refused to clear his plane for a mission because the guns were defective. He was overruled by the pilot. During an attack by enemy fighters the cocking mechanism on the guns broke and the gunner was forced to cock the guns using the red hot stub bolt that held the cockling toggle on. His hands were burnt to the bone but his actions saved the plane. For that action the pilot was awarded the DFC, the co-pilot received an honourable mention and the gunner was ignored.

Colour sergeant Frank Bourne turned down his VC. The reason is as follows: in the film the scene is where C/Sgt Bourne is against the sandbags the officer says 'Colour Sargent " CSgt Bourne looks up and replies "yes sir, I was sleeping" it is because of this he felt that he had let his regiment down. He declined to accept the VC. He continued to get promoted to the rank of lieutenant Colonel.

Pvt. Cole actually lived through the battle but did not answer roll call, as he was buried under the hospital with Hooks, and was later listed as MIA in the rolls. In fact, Bourne later helped Cole with his kit, as while you could be promoted in the British army, they would not pay you if you were listed as dead. Cole also became an officer and eventually received pay, and he may have outlived Bourne as well. I knew his great-grandson Alan Cole jr., who was Captain of Brigade in Rhodesia, and his father was killed on the San Pueblo spy ship when it was captured. I believe the Cole's had their own coat of arms (a bear?), and I think another Cole ancestor is in a painting overthrowing some Noble, and was later hanged for his troubles.

The real Colour Sergeant Bourne was not the elderly father-figure as shown in the movie - he was just 24 years old. He was also rather short. OBE is "Officer of the Order of the British Empire". MBE is Member of the Order, etc. The first letter denotes the rank in the order. You cannot be awarded "The Order of the British Empire".

zuuuuluuuuu!   zuuuuuluuuuu!  The Henry was a single shot breach loader.  Surely a step up from a muzzle loader, being loaded with what any modern rifleman would recognise as a 'round' today.  Still, the shear courage it would take to go into battle with such a weapon is beyond understanding, since the bayonet was an essential addition, not the curio "combat knife that happens to have a mount for the muzzle of your automatic carbine" that it is today.  Only the best trained troops would be able to do well with it when the odds are worse than 40 to 1.  Remember this was a rear-guard unit too.  The British army really took training seriously and still do today I believe...  training Ukrainian batteries to fight the Russians in an artillery war.  Damn proud of my heritage.  It was history like this that encouraged many "Tommies" to join up in 1914, but alas they were met with the realities of the new trench warfare, something that mostly showed no respect for shear guts.  We will probably never see the likes of Frank Bourne again, though his dedication to duty is as exemplary now as it was then.

Witness accounts tell of the 2 lieutenants being spotted trying to saddle horses and run away, their fellow officers described them as useless. The real defence was lead by the commisary and colour sergeant Bourne. The establishment decided that the officers should get victoria crosses and that the real heroes should het minor awards as they were of the wrong class- an attitude typical of the time. Following a huge uproar in the press, it was finally decided that the commisary should receive a victoria cross and Bourne the DSO. Money talks, merit walks as the saying goes

I joined the Australian Army in 1962 and was posted to the Royal Australian Regiment (Infantry) on graduation.  I lied about my age to enlist as I was only 15 years old at the time and should have been 17 years old.  I had completed my enlistment papers but left my date of birth out.  My dad who had to counter sign the form noticed what I had done and said: "Oi, you have left out your date of birth."  I told dad to sign the form and I would fill out the details later.  My dear old Irish mother was horrified when she asked me what date of birth I had give the Army.  I told her I had given the date of June 1944 (when in reality, my date of birth was June 1946.  Quick as a flash, mum responded: 'WHAT HAVE YOU DONE TO ME REPUTATION (in her quaint Irish accent) I WASN'T EVEN MARRIED THEN."  Mum and dad were married in September 1944.  As a former  Australian  Soldier, the funniest line I heard used (I am unsure whether or not it was really used of written into the film) was at that point after the Zulus  withdrew, the roll was called  which is a standard procedure.  C/Sgt. Bourne calling the roll came to Private Hitch.  He called Hitch's name twice and got no response.  C/Sgt. Bourne called out a third time.  "HITCH, YOU ARE ALIVE.  I SAW YOU."  Private Hitch then replies with words to the effect: "Oh Yes" .  C/Sgt Bourne responds.  "SAY SIR; OFFICER ON PARADE"  It has been years since I watched this film.  I hope I got the sequence of things correct.  Thank you kindly for an excellent programme.

I struggle with emotions on all of this. I do admire the individual courage and self discipline of the enlisted personnel and NCOs. However, consider why they were there and why were they under attack.....they were invaders and the native population was trying to repulse their domination. Soldiers don't typically get to pick the battles. But they are judged for their character and heroism in battle regardless of the bigger right or wrong that their leaders created.  We have similar disparity of values in the U.S. One of the many examples exists near where I live. There is a park honoring the gallant heroism of a young officer in the19th century. He won great acclaim and admiration from his superiors for his courage and leadership in battle with the enemy. Who was the enemy? It was farmers and fishermen who were desperately resisting having their families and livelihoods forcibly displaced yet again to land even less desirable to their needs than the land they were defending.

Thanks for the excellent piece of biography. As far as a request for a video goes, I do have one. I served for quite some time in the Australian Army, and during some historical reading on the Second Battle of Villers-Bretonneux, mention is made in the sources I was reading of extraordinarily skilled action by British cavalry in both screening and providing reconnaissance for Australia forces. However, no mention was made of what regiment/s were involved, or exactly what they accomplished. Nonetheless, the action of cavalry in this battle was vital to the eventual victory that helped stop the 1918 offensive. Perhaps this is a small thing amongst British feats of arms, but as a vital action in a war where the value of cavalry is often derided, I think it would be an interesting little backwater to explore. For your consideration.

An interesting video. One point regarding C/Sgt Bourneâ€™s account of Rorkesdrift highlighted in the video; you can listen to it on YouTube and it is actually read by Bourneâ€™s grandson.  Bourne makes the point, in his account, that the Zulus who attacked the mission station used the rifles seized earlier from the men of the 24th Foot killed at Isandhlwana.  This is incorrect. As the â€œHistory Manâ€ correctly states, the Zulus who attacked Rorkesdrift were not the same men who attacked Isandhlwana. The former were from Impis who had been kept in reserve.  So even an eye witness to the conflict can get things wrong!

Hi History Chap, my favourite line from this movie is "Mr. Dewitt sir, be quiet now will you, there's a good gentleman!"
Informative and 'era correct' in your approach to explain the 'mystery' behind no VC for the 'Color Sergeant.  As a retired and miltary service connected 'disabled' senior non-commissioned officer, I recognized his 'award' in his words you quoted.  His men were a direct reflection of his 'leadership qualities and influence in their welfare and care. That is every NCO's 'award'.  Done successfully, they are enabled to continue their military careers and receive further promotions to higher positions of responsibility in troop leadership.  As a fairly new but 'novice' on British history, particularly that of 'The Raj' and the East India Company and it's 'colonization' of India.  I've learned a lot about British demeanor, attitudes and expectations projected through those who fought the several 'tribes' and then those who begat the India Civil Service, in their own image.  Even from the begining, these 'attitudes' and expectations' were projected in achieving what they sought most.  English trade in India.  'The Color Sergeant' was trained by this tradition that went back to days before his own birth.  These were the days of 'Empire'.  Days that the sun never set on the British Empire.   I should think that the fears that plagued the 'The Raj', dealing with perceived threats to it's 'commerce' from the Russians would make EXCELLENT subject matter. As this lasted from the early 18th century, up through the Afhan Massacres, the Indian Mutiny, there will be plenty of 'history' to be related into several chapters/videos.  My interest in 'The Raj' stemmed from US President Joe Bidens' sudden 'surrender' in Afghanistan.  I'd earlier read a book given to me by my brother, then in the US Department of State, Peter Hopkirk's 'The Great Game: The Struggle for Empire in Central Asia.  I'd remembered the massacres and determined I needed to 'refresh' my memory and now 'understand why'.. Essentially The British, The Russians, and The Americans have all 'been there' and done that with varying degrees of success, short term or long term.  My desire to understand this 'dynamic' led me to Lawrence James's 'The Raj'; and subsequently to this comment about the famous 'Color Sergeant'.

It's probably worth noting that no senior NCO in his right mind would want to become a junior officer.  Particularly given the need at that time for a private income. What most people don't know is that Lieutenant Bromhead was almost totally deaf, had a huge beard and moustache (As most soldiers did in those days) and the officers had to purchase their own pistols, instead of being issued with them. My late grandfather was an army orphan/sutler's boy/bugler attached to a Royal Navy detachment that enfiladed a Zulu Kraal at the same time as Rourke's Drift was being attacked. He was stabbed in the stomach by two Zulus with asegais, survived, went on to fight in both Boer Wars and the First World War, and tried to re-enlist in the British Army at the start of WW2, but was deemed too old, which he raged about for quite a while, according to my father, who was called up for duty in 1939. My dad always said my grandfather would rather die fighting than in bed, and he did, as he was also an Army bare knuckle boxing champion, and continued fighting bouts privately long after he retired from the Army, and had his last fight the day he died. They just don't seem to make men like these anymore :-) My grandfather was born in the back of an army wagon, was raised by the Army, and missed his life among his comrades in arms long after he was forced to retire from the army in the 1920's. I remember watching Zulu and Zulu Dawn over the years, and read the wonderful book "The Washing Of The Spears" about what really happened during the Anglo-Zulu Wars, and the movie showed Private Hook wrongly, as he was one of the bravest men at Rourke's Drift, and later became a Colour Sergeant himself, and it was his character that they based Colour Sergeant Bourne on for the movie :-) I still love both movies, and watch them often. The later stories of those brave men are even more interesting than most people think, as many of them went on to have even more adventures in their careers as soldiers, and after they left the army.

As an ex Natalian, it was very interesting visiting these sites as a teenager.  Many others were there.  Little outposts in Zululand on the tops of Koppies (hills).  There were little military cemeteries with regimental graves.  They were completely untended then.  Grown over.  I heard many were later disturbed / robbed for tunic buttons, etc.  Very sadly!  Almost all of the soldiers died of disease.  As was mainly the case all over colonial Africa and Asia. Probably only a minority fell in combat.

I have actually fired a Martini-Henry rifle. It was, and still is, not a very pleasant experience. For those soldiers to have maintained any sort of rate of fire  was amazing. After about 20 rounds the barrel would have started to become very hot and would also have started to become quite foul. Given that 100-odd soldiers fired off about 20,000 rounds of "reserve" ammunition, that's over 260 rounds each counting the 60 rounds per man of normal use cartridges, that's a heck of a lot of fouling. No wonder the Zulu casualties were on the low side.

I would like to see an account of the battle of Quebec - General Wolfe ordered his troops to ascend the Heights of Abraham, easy to type, but a task that would seem almost impossible, even today.  Once done, the 35th of foot, faced the elite French Rousillon Regiment on the right of the line, and basically, slaughtered them.  The 35th of foot...'the orange lilys', became the 1st Battalion Royal Sussex, who would later become the 3rd Battalion The Queen's Regiment, in which I served.  The 35th of foot (featured in the film 'The last of the Mohicans' ) had previous history with the French, and were determined to take revenge on the French for the betrayal featured in that film. I would very much like to hear more about this battle and the role of the 35th, as I don't think it's been done.

Jeff Cooper writes of a heroic fight where the Zulus are trying to come in the back door of the hospital. An officer told a private--"Hold that door"--Yes Sir!! With fixed bayonet he killed 16 Zulus in single combat before being overcome due to exhaustion after which he was killed. Instead of entering the hospital the Zulus stepped back and performed a king of brief "dance" which they used to honor a particularly worthy adversary and then poured into the building. This is not shown in the movie.

Chris Morning, Really enjoyed this one, great research and enthusiasm!  - Bourne's comments to the Padre "Mr. Witt sir... be quiet now will you, there's a good gentleman, you'll upset the lads." - It encapsulates the very essence of the recognition of a senior NCO, having to balance the discipline, brawn and brain over the delicacies of the Victorian class system - a well-constructed scene which tells us a lot about the challenges at that time for very capable individuals, but lovely to see he ended up as a half colonel and a CBE - possibly the best success story of the men of Rorkes Drift.  It would be interesting to know if his kids were able to escape the poverty/ class trap and entered British society with better prospects and where was he laid to rest?

Chris, what an amazing topic. When I joined the army [1980] the movie was a must watch prior to attending your first promotion course for corporal. And this was 1980 and only a couple of people in the lines [barracks] had a video player. So you had to sign out the tape out from the OIC training and then we as a group [5or 6] would cram into a room in the lines to watch and re watch the movie. There was no History Chap and YouTube. We were in a rage when we saw that he did not get the VC. And no one knew this amazing story. He was a brilliant soldier and they are few and far between. Just in the past week I have purchased some brand new copies of the movie as well as Zulu Dawn. Not for me to watch as I have on my hard drive. Just in case I loose my copy, they will remain in their clear wraps. Colour Sargent Bourne was an inspiration to myself and many young soldiers over the decades, Cheers love your video's

My first CSM, a Coldstream guardsman from Yorkshire reminded me of C/sgt Bourne from the first day I met him as a young recruit in 1976. A no- nonsense, brick shithouse of a man he exemplified professionalism, patience and honesty. I would have numerous CSM's in the following 25 years but he was the only one whom I never heard swear or show anger. I can still hear him say, "You're like a ruddy teddy bear lad", when I fecked up on the parade square. Good man.

As an historical representation I have become less enchanted by the film since more of the historical inaccuracies came to light, such as the claimed Welsh nature of 24th at that time, and the alleged presence of the Company choir (led by Ivor Emmanuel whose film career the rÃ´le was supposed to boost).  Only later did the 24th become the South Wales Borderers; but Stanley Baker apparently insisted that they be Welsh.  Further, the salute by the Zulus after the fighting on film, did not happen, although I can certainly see why a device was found to commend the Zulu bravery.  However, Nigel Green's performance was outstanding.  I like his " Because we're 'ere, lad, because we're 'ere, lad.... Just us..... nobody else", which gives his realistic prediction of the likely outcome.  Similarly, his human but disciplined approach to the roll call parade, is beautifully done.  Best of all, though, for my money, is his conversation with the drunken Jack Hawkins: it is a style of deferential yet forceful insistence which I have seen deployed by Mess Sar'n Majors when young officers had been overindulging on the bottle and were likely to start damaging the Mess. Utterly brilliant!

Thank you - another very interesting video!  My favourite Colour Sgt Bourne quote is the one you mentioned - "Because we're 'ere lad - and no one else.  Just us."  He also says the quote that has been wrongly attributed to Michael Caine by one or two impressionists in the past: "...Zulus... - thousands of them!"  Except he says "The sentries report Zulus to the south west - thousands of them."  And I've just remembered another Colour Sgt Bourne quote:  Near the beginning of the film, Bromhead is out hunting, and a rifle shot in the distance is heard at the Drift.  Hughes (he of the bushy mutton chops and moustache) hangs out the hospital window and asks "Colour Sergeant Bourne, what's that shooting?" "A rifle, Hughes" comes Bourne's reply!  Great stuff.  The rock formation that is the dramatic backdrop to the film is called "The Amphitheatre" and if you were to climb that escarpment, you'd more or less be in Lesotho at the top.  It makes for a great background, but is actually about 100 miles to the south west of the real Rorke's Drift.  It's a fantastic location though!
